    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1432764511" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1432764511" />
    <a title="Stephanie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5975841-stephanie"><img alt="Stephanie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1469205584p2/5975841.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1432764511?book_show_action=true">Nov 03, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stephanie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stephanie" href="/user/show/5975841-stephanie">Stephanie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5975841-stephanie?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5975841-stephanie?shelf=read-and-gave-away">read-and-gave-away</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5975841-stephanie?shelf=here-there-be-dragons">here-there-be-dragons</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1432764511" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2574130597904360937">  I read the first 200 or so pages of this while on a trip, then waited nearly 3 weeks to finish reading it. All told, I think I preferred the first 200 pages the most and therefore sped joyfully through those, though there were still some very good moments scattered throughout the rest of the book. Despite the threat of the Darkwood, for some reason, I had the most fun reading the “short story” of Rupert saving Julia from the dragon and their initial time back at the palace.<br/><br/>  Overall, this is a</span>
  <span id="freeText2574130597904360937" style="display:none">  I read the first 200 or so pages of this while on a trip, then waited nearly 3 weeks to finish reading it. All told, I think I preferred the first 200 pages the most and therefore sped joyfully through those, though there were still some very good moments scattered throughout the rest of the book. Despite the threat of the Darkwood, for some reason, I had the most fun reading the “short story” of Rupert saving Julia from the dragon and their initial time back at the palace.<br /><br />  Overall, this is a realistic-leaning heroic adventure story, rife with intense battles against the Dark, shady political dealings and back-stabbings, as well as a healthful dose of wry humor and outright hilarity. I found the tone to be a mix of the usual “fantasy realm” and a sort of modern awareness – with certain jokes feeling out of place in a fantasy-historical setting, but making complete sense to the modern reader. (While not the greatest example, it is the first one that pops into mind: where Rupert briefly wonders what an aardvark is after the High Warlock threatens to turn him into one. Or something like that.) It made for a refreshing read, and something a little different than what I am normally used to in my fantasy tales.<br /><br />  Side note: I have an entire section below for the typos I found while reading this 2005 softcover edition – which was far more than I like to see even in a book of this length. I hope that any subsequent editions had these things corrected...<br /><br />  In the end, I enjoyed the beginning of the story the most, was entertained throughout the rest of it, and it came to an ending tied up nicely in a pretty jewel-encrusted bow. A little too neatly, in my honest opinion (but then, I also just came off of finishing the <u>Animorphs</u> series, and that sets a pretty darn high bar for any ending...). It seems that this book is the first in a sort of series – while the world intrigues me, and I had fun in this book, I do not see myself making a point to revisit the world in future books. I like it as a one-off, and am content in having read it as a one-off.<br /><br />Favorite quotes and commentary upon them where applicable:<br /><br />  “Well,” said Rupert awkwardly, “It’d look rather bad it [sic] I came back riding the unicorn while you had to walk. You’d better ride him the rest of the way.”<br />   “No thanks,” said Julia.<br />   “I really think it would be better…”<br />   “No,” said Julia firmly.<br />   “Why not?”<br />   “Because I <i>can’t</i> ride a unicorn, that’s why not!”<br />  Rupert looked at the ground, and scuffed some dirt with his boot.<br />   “Oh,” he said finally.<br />   “And what’s that supposed to mean?”<br />   “It means he’s thinking,” said the unicorn. “Always a bad sign.”<br />   “So I’m not qualified to ride a unicorn,” said Julia. “Big deal.”<br />   “Unfortunately, around here it is,” said Rupert. “Unicorn; you’ve gone lame.”<br />   “No, I haven’t,” said the unicorn.<br />   “Yes, you have,” said Rupert. “That’s why both Julia and I are walking.”<br />   “I suppose you want me to limp,” said the unicorn.<br />   “Got it in one,” said Rupert. – page 60 – I got this right away, but I had to explain to my coworker (who concurrently read the first 200 pages with me), that only virgins can ride unicorns according to legend.<br /><br />   [Rupert] sheathed his sword, and hurried over just in time to stop Julia slamming a boot into the Lady Cecelia.<br />   “Julia, not here! Please, come with me to the Court and meet my father. There are lots of people there you can hit, I’ll be happy to point them out to you, but don’t waste your spleen on amateurs like these. The real creeps wouldn’t be seen dead outside the Court.” – page 71<br /><br />  The King smiled grimly. If he couldn’t have loyalty, he’d settle for fear. He had a war to wage, and with the darkness pressing closer all the time he could no longer afford to be choosy over which weapons he used. – page 149 – Never a good thought process for a leader to have… it can only lead down dark roads…<br /><br />   “I’m tired, Thomas. We’ll talk about this tomorrow.”<br />   “John…”<br />   “Tomorrow, Thomas.”<br />  The Astrologer watched the King walk slowly across his empty Court. “Tomorrow may be too late, John,” he said quietly, but if the king heard him, he gave no sign to show it. – page 156 – this is probably where I second had my suspicions (the first being Thomas’ little lightning trick on Sir Bedivere).<br /><br />   “[Gregory is s]till having problems from when Julia [kneed him in the groin], but I’m helping to cure that.”<br />  Darius chuckled, and Cecelia smiled at him affectionately. “Dear Darius. Sometimes I wish…”<br />   “I’m sorry,” said Darius. “But you know I’ve never been interested in that sort of thing.” – page 168 – I had to detail to my friend what this exchange was implying -- or at least one interpretation of it – which is that Darius is gay (yes, the same friend I had to explain the ‘only a virgin can ride a unicorn’ bit to).<br /><br />   [The High Warlock offered Rupert some wine, which Rupert declined.] “It’s good stuff,” insisted the Warlock. “I brew it myself.” He waved his free hand at the glass tubing, and then leaned forward confidentially. “I put a dead rat in every new barrel, to give it a little body.”<br />  Rupert decided not to think about that. – page 221 – Yuck! (but funny)<br /><br />   [The High Warlock] sniffed and shrugged, and walked toward the nearest window. “You know, Rupert; I should never have left the Sorcerers’ Academy. I was quite happy there, changing gold into lead.”<br />   “Shouldn’t that be lead into gold?” asked Rupert.<br />   “Why do you think I had to leave?” said the High Warlock. – page 229<br /><br />  Julia glared at him. “Look, Harald, I’m going to say this once and once only, so listen carefully. I don’t love you. I don’t like you. I have about as much feeling for you as I have for what they shovel out of the stables every morning. I wouldn’t marry you if the alternative was leprosy. Got it?” – page 248 – And this little bit is why her actions much later confused me so.<br /><br />   “I don’t love Harald!” [Julia said to the King.]<br />   “You don’t have to. In a royal marriage, duty is more important than love. And don’t frown like that, as though duty was an ugly word; it is, but we can’t escape it. Just by being born into royalty, you and I took on responsibilities along with our privileges. We get the best of everything because we have the hardest work to do. We live in luxury because we give up everything else that matters. We weigh ourselves down with duty so that others can be free. And unlike any other job, we can’t walk away if the work gets too hard, or we don’t want to do it.” – page 256<br /><br />   “Do you love me?” [Cecelia said to Gregory.]<br />   “Perhaps. What difference does it make, as long as we’re having a good time together? Worry about tomorrow when it comes; for now, we have each other, and I’ve never been happier. Never.” – page 275<br /><br />   [Rupert and the unicorn and their small group have just exited the Darkwood] “Not much longer now,” [Rupert] muttered gruffly. “One last stretch of the legs, and then we can all take a rest.”<br />   “You keep telling me that,” said the unicorn dourly. “A nice long rest in a warm, dry stable…I’ll believe it when I see it, and not before. I just hope they’ve got some decent food. I’ve had nothing but grass for weeks. I think I’d kill for some barely.”<br />   “Once we’re safely in the Castle, I’ll bury you in barley.”<br />   “Given our present situation, I don’t think that remark is in the best of taste.”<br />  Rupert and the unicorn shared a look, and then chuckled quietly together. – page 303<br /><br />   [A man came running up to Rupert upon his return to the Castle from fetching the High Warlock, requesting his pardon as he came immediately upon hearing Rupert was back.] “You did?” said Rupert.<br />   “But of course, Sire! You have come back to us out of the very darkness itself; come to save us all! What a song I shall make of this!”<br />  Rupert looked at him. “A song?” he said, slowly.<br />   “Well yes, Sire. I’m the new official Court minstrel. But not to worry, Sire, the song I shall make of your daring exploits will be a tale of great heroes and selfless deeds, of honor and adventure and miraculous escapes…”<br />  His voice trailed away as he caught sight of Rupert’s face. He started backing away and [sic] when Rupert drew his sword, and then turned and ran as Rupert advanced on him with murder in his eyes. Rupert gave up after a few steps, but the minstrel had the good sense to keep running.<br />   “Was that really necessary?” asked the unicorn.<br />   “Definitely,” growled Rupert, sheathing his sword and leaning back against the Castle wall. “It was minstrels and their damn stupid songs on the joys of adventuring that got me into this mess in the first place.” – page 321 – Especially funny considering in the early part of the book he was complaining about minstrels and how they only sing of the glorious bits, not the mundane or painful things like going to the bathroom in full armor or getting sliced by a demon claw.<br /><br />   “I hadn’t realized it had been so long [since I had changed you into a something],” said the Warlock. “I’m sorry. I’ll change you back…”<br />  <i>“No! Please; no. I’m happy here, guarding the moat. It’s all I want; all I ever wanted. In the summer there are fish and birds and insects, and I hear their voices, hear their songs. The wind and the rain and the Forest are a part of me now, and I am a part of them. I can feel the seasons change, and the world turn, and the slow steady pulse of the living. I can’t give that up. I can’t go back to being human. To being only human.</i> -- page 337 – No, that initial quote mark is not a typo on my part. But I really like this little passage, the honesty of it, the emotion of it. How just a man became so much more, and became so in tune with Nature that he did not want to go back to being “only human.”<br /><br />   “Magic is going out of the world, Rupert; and all because of us.”<br />   “Us?” said Rupert.<br />   “Man,” said the Warlock. “All because of Man. His logical, rational mind will be the death of magic yet. Magic works by its own rules, and they don’t pay much attention to cause and effect. That’s why all the truly great sorcerers have always been eccentrics; they mastered magic because they were as whimsical and contradictory as the sorcery they studied. Magic has its own structure and logic, but even those tend to be contracts of agreement rather than natural laws. I’m confusing you, aren’t I? Magic’s a confusing business. Every year there are less and less people who can bend their minds enough to control magic. Fewer and fewer mad enough to understand sorcery, while still sane enough not to be destroyed by it.<br />  All too soon, magic will be gone from this world, Rupert; driven out by Man, with his need for logic and reason and simple, understandable answers. Science will replace magic, and we’ll all be a damn sight better off. Science always works. All we’ll have lost will be a little poetry, a little beauty… and perhaps a little of the wonder of the world. No more dragons. No more unicorns, or goblins, or wee folk.” – page 382 – This is possibly my favorite passage from this entire book.<br /><br /><br /><u>Typographical errors (non-exhaustive) :</u><br /><br />“It’d look rather bad it I came back…” – page 60 – “if” instead of “it”<br /><br />“…might I suggest hat once the demons’…” – page 151 – “hat” instead of “that”<br /><br />“…give a damn about the demons; they’ll be to busy worrying…” – page 154 – “to” instead of “too”<br /><br />“…And once we’ve place him on the throne, he’ll soon find…” – page 161 – “place” instead of “placed”<br /><br />“…of the darkness in their hundreds and swarmed…” – page 209 – “the” instead of “their”<br /><br />“…their cheering excitement of only a few moment before gone, as…” – page 317 – “moments” instead of “moment”<br /><br /><i>“No! Please; no….</i> -- page 337 -- Erroneous opening quotation which is never closed either.<br /><br />“But does anybody listen? Do they hell as like.” – page 362 – Unless this is something dialectical, because the second sentence is practically gibberish to me…<br /><br />“Other clutched at their throats and fell…” – page 409 – “Others” instead of “Other”<br /><br />“…outlining the rotting trees with its shark brilliance, and…” – page 421 – “stark” instead of “shark”<br /><br />“…his schemes came unstuck, and then forget where he hid it…” – page 448 – “forget” instead of “forgot”<br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[“…waited patiently while […] his eyes adjusted to the sudden change in light.” – page 468 – “eye” instead of “eyes” – he did lose his right eye in the battle, after all, as noted on page 432. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br /><u>Consistent “typos” – actually the difference between American English and British English?</u><br /><br />“…into rotten wood was eerily loud on the quiet, but…” – page 207 – “in” instead of “on”<br /><br />“…bloodred sun, hanging low on the sky, and tried…” – page 215 – “in” instead of “on”<br /><br />“…the slow, regular sound eerily loud on the silence.” – page 261 – “in” instead of “on” –unless this is a dialectical phrase, since it has happened three times at least already…<br /><br />“…eerily distinct on the quiet.” – page 357 – “in” instead of “on”<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="2574130597904360937" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2490531804" />
    <a title="Danny" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4493860-danny"><img alt="Danny" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1446067273p2/4493860.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2490531804?book_show_action=true">Aug 30, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Danny" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Danny" href="/user/show/4493860-danny">Danny</a>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('2auuVB+1oVb1S2K56zxygqRzHggfy6xMIF1FhO3OSFiUx/9J5xBpF1+PL+tzk/qT05RwS679tcT7B6Ao9Fyhcw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

<span id="freeTextContainer16875243173665373671"><b>Simon Richard Green</b> is a British science fiction and fantasy-author. He holds a degree in Modern English and American Literature from the University of Leicester. His first publication was in 1979.<br/><br/>His Deathstalker series is partly a parody of the usual space-opera of the 1950s, told with sovereign disregard of the rules of probability, while being at the same time extremely bloodthirsty.<br/><br/><i>Excerpted </i></span>
  <span id="freeText16875243173665373671" style="display:none"><b>Simon Richard Green</b> is a British science fiction and fantasy-author. He holds a degree in Modern English and American Literature from the University of Leicester.
